The Philosophers' Proof for Divine Unity
Philosophers' argument that two necessary existents would be composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that positing two necessary existents leads to problematic composition, thus proving divine unity.

Core Idea
Two necessary existents would share in necessity but differ by individuation—each would be composite, which is impossible.

In this lesson, we explore the first proof for God's oneness as presented by the philosophers. They argue that if there were two necessary beings, each would be composed of a shared attribute (necessity) and a unique particularity (individuation). This composition is impossible for a necessary being, so only one necessary existent can exist. Let's examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is part of a larger discussion on proofs for God's oneness.
2
Identify the key concepts
Find the terms 'وجوب ذاتي' (essential necessity) and 'تعين'/'تشخص' (individuation/particularization). Understand that they are distinct.
3
Trace the logical structure
The argument goes: suppose two necessary existents → they share in necessity → they differ by individuation → each is composed of both → composition is impossible for a necessary being.
The conclusion of impossibility is stated but the reasons come in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why did the philosophers think this leads to God's oneness? How does composition undermine necessary existence?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا الواحد اعلم أن الناس ذكروا أنواعا من الدلائل على وحدانية الله تعالى، ونحن نذكرها على الاستقصاء: أما الحجة الأولى: التي عليها تعويل الفلاسفة فهي أن قالوا: لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته، لكانا متشاركين في المفهوم من الوجوب الذاتي، ولا بد وأن يختلفا بالتعين والتشخص، إذ لو كان كون هذا حاصلا لذاك، لكان هذا عين ذاك، ولكان ذاك عين هذا. وحينئذ يكون الكل شيئا واحدا، فيثبت أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته، لوجب كونها متشاركين في الوجوب الذاتي، وكون كل واحد منهما [للآخر في نفسه وتشخصه، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة فيكون كل واحد منها] مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره، ومن التعين الذي به يخالف غيره، وهذا محال لوجوه: الأول: إن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره، فكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى غيره [وكل مفتقر إلى غيره] فإنه ممكن، فثبت أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته، لوجب كون كل واحد منهما ممكنا لذاته، وما أفضى ثبوته إلى عدمه كان باطلا، فيثبت أن فرض موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته: فرض باطل محال فيثبت أن وجود موجودين واجبي الوجود قول باطل.
On the fact that the Necessary Existent in itself is only one. Know that people have mentioned types of proofs for the oneness of God, and we shall mention them comprehensively. As for the first proof: upon which the philosophers rely, it is that they said: If we suppose two existents, each of which is necessary in itself, they would share in the concept of essential necessity, and they must necessarily differ by individuation and particularization, for if the existence of this one were identical to that one, this one would be that one, and that one would be this one. Then the whole would be one thing. Thus it is established that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary in itself, they must share in essential necessity, and each one is [different from the other in itself and its particularization, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so each one] would be composed of the necessity by which it shares with another, and of the individuation by which it differs from another. This is impossible for several aspects: First: Every composite is dependent on each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it, so every composite is dependent on another, and everything dependent on another is contingent. Thus it is established that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary in itself, each one would necessarily be contingent in itself, and [the supposition] whose establishment leads to its own negation is false. Thus it is established that the supposition of two existents each of which is necessary in itself is a false and impossible supposition, and thus it is established that the existence of two necessary existents is a false statement.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a proof for God's oneness. Razi first states that he will present various proofs. The first proof is from the philosophers. They argue that if there were two necessary existents, they would share the attribute of necessity but differ in their specific identity (individuation). If they were identical in all respects, they would be one, so they must differ in something. Therefore each necessary existent would be composed of a shared element (necessity) and a distinguishing element (individuation). Composition is impossible for a necessary being. Razi then presents the first of three objections: a composite depends on its parts, and dependence on another indicates contingency. Thus each supposed necessary being would be contingent, which is contradictory. Therefore the original supposition is false.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof argues that if there were two necessary existents, each would be composite, which is incompatible with necessary existence.

The First Refutation: Composition Implies Contingency
Razi's first objection: composition implies dependence, hence contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first objection that composition implies dependence, which implies contingency, thus refuting the philosophers' argument.

Core Idea
Every composite depends on its parts, and dependence on another is a sign of contingency, which a necessary being cannot have.

Having presented the philosophers' argument, Razi now provides the first of several objections. He argues that composition itself entails dependence—something a necessary being cannot have. This objection effectively undermines the assumption that two necessary existents could be composite. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that it begins by calling the composition impossible for several aspects, then gives the first.
2
Follow the logical chain
Trace: every composite depends on its parts → parts are other than the composite → dependence on something other than itself → that dependence indicates contingency.
This is a syllogistic argument.
3
Understand the contradiction
If the supposed necessary being is contingent, then the original assumption (two necessary existents) leads to its own negation, proving it false.
4
Connect to the previous lesson
How does this objection refute the philosophers' proof? The proof assumed composition was impossible without proof; now Razi shows why composition is impossible because it leads to contingency.
This completes the first refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا الواحد اعلم أن الناس ذكروا أنواعا من الدلائل على وحدانية الله تعالى، ونحن نذكرها على الاستقصاء: أما الحجة الأولى: التي عليها تعويل الفلاسفة فهي أن قالوا: لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته، لكانا متشاركين في المفهوم من الوجوب الذاتي، ولا بد وأن يختلفا بالتعين والتشخص، إذ لو كان كون هذا حاصلا لذاك، لكان هذا عين ذاك، ولكان ذاك عين هذا. وحينئذ يكون الكل شيئا واحدا، فيثبت أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته، لوجب كونها متشاركين في الوجوب الذاتي، وكون كل واحد منهما [للآخر في نفسه وتشخصه، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة فيكون كل واحد منها] مركبا من الوجوب الذي به يشارك غيره، ومن التعين الذي به يخالف غيره، وهذا محال لوجوه: الأول: إن كل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل واحد من أجزائه غيره، فكل مركب فإنه مفتقر إلى غيره [وكل مفتقر إلى غيره] فإنه ممكن، فثبت أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته، لوجب كون كل واحد منهما ممكنا لذاته، وما أفضى ثبوته إلى عدمه كان باطلا، فيثبت أن فرض موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته: فرض باطل محال فيثبت أن وجود موجودين واجبي الوجود قول باطل.
On the fact that the Necessary Existent in itself is only one. Know that people have mentioned types of proofs for the oneness of God, and we shall mention them comprehensively. As for the first proof: upon which the philosophers rely, it is that they said: If we suppose two existents, each of which is necessary in itself, they would share in the concept of essential necessity, and they must necessarily differ by individuation and particularization, for if the existence of this one were identical to that one, this one would be that one, and that one would be this one. Then the whole would be one thing. Thus it is established that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary in itself, they must share in essential necessity, and each one is [different from the other in itself and its particularization, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so each one] would be composed of the necessity by which it shares with another, and of the individuation by which it differs from another. This is impossible for several aspects: First: Every composite is dependent on each of its parts, and each of its parts is other than it, so every composite is dependent on another, and everything dependent on another is contingent. Thus it is established that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary in itself, each one would necessarily be contingent in itself, and [the supposition] whose establishment leads to its own negation is false. Thus it is established that the supposition of two existents each of which is necessary in itself is a false and impossible supposition, and thus it is established that the existence of two necessary existents is a false statement.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a proof for God's oneness. Razi first states that he will present various proofs. The first proof is from the philosophers. They argue that if there were two necessary existents, they would share the attribute of necessity but differ in their specific identity (individuation). If they were identical in all respects, they would be one, so they must differ in something. Therefore each necessary existent would be composed of a shared element (necessity) and a distinguishing element (individuation). Composition is impossible for a necessary being. Razi then presents the first of three objections: a composite depends on its parts, and dependence on another indicates contingency. Thus each supposed necessary being would be contingent, which is contradictory. Therefore the original supposition is false.

Key Terms
مركب
مفتقر
إمكان
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of Razi's first objection?
A) Every composite depends on its parts.
B) Two necessary existents are impossible.
C) Individuation causes composition.
D) The philosophers' argument is sound.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first objection shows that composition entails dependence, and dependence entails contingency, which makes the philosophers' assumption self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every composite depends on its parts, and you are composite, then you depend on parts that are not you. Where do you place your ultimate trust—on these parts or on the One who is not composed?
?
Razi shows that dependency entails contingency. Look at your life: in what areas do you still act as if you are self-sufficient? How does this lesson expose that illusion?
?
If only the Absolutely Simple can be Necessary, then your own existence is a constant reminder of your need for Allah. When you feel self-reliant, what practical step can you take today to break that feeling and affirm your contingency?

Second Refutation: Infinite Regress of Composition
Razi presents a regress argument against composite necessary existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second refutation against multiple necessary existents, which shows that assuming multiple necessary existents leads to an infinite regress of parts and contradicts the simplicity of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
If a necessary existent has parts, those parts must also be necessary, leading to infinite regress.

Razi continues his critique of the view that there could be more than one necessary existent. In this second objection, he argues that if a supposed necessary being is composed of parts (sharing necessity and differing by particularity), then those parts themselves must be necessary, and the same reasoning applies recursively, leading to an infinite regress. This shows the impossibility of a composite necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the compressed style and logical structure.
2
Identify the initial dichotomy
The argument begins by dividing the parts of a composite necessary existent into two categories: necessary in themselves or possible in themselves.
This is a crucial step for the reductio.
3
Follow the possibility of a part being possible
If any part is possible, then the composite depends on that part, and the composite would become possible, contradicting its necessity.
4
Trace the regress when each part is necessary
If each part is necessary, then each part itself shares in necessity and differs by particularity, making each part composite in the same way, leading to an infinite number of parts.
This is the core of the refutation.
5
Understand the conclusion
The infinite regress shows that the supposed 'one' is not truly one, contradicting the nature of a necessary existent, thus refuting the multiplicity.
Razi concludes that the original assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إنه إما أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء واجب الوجود لذاته، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك والقسم الثاني باطل، لأن المركب مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، فلو كان [شيء من أجزائه] ممكنا لذاته أنه يكون مفتقرا إلى [ذلك الجزء، لزم كونه في ذاته مفتقرا إلى] الممكن لذاته، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان، فيلزم أن يكون [الواجب لذاته، ممكنا لذاته]. وذلك محال فيثبت: أن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء يكون [واجبا لذاته]، فتلك الأجزاء متشاركة في الوجوب الذاتي، ومتباينة بخصوصياتها التي باعتبارها يخالف بعضها بعضا، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة، فيلزم كون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء مركبا [من جزئين آخرين، والكلام في تلك الاجزاء كما في الأول، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما مركب] من أجزاء غير متناهية وذلك محال. لأن كل كثرة متناهية أو غير متناهية [فإن الواحد فيها موجود إلا أن أي شيء صدق، يحكم العقل بأنه واحد وهذا في نفسه ليس بواحد، لأن ذلك الواحد لابد وأن يشارك سائر الأجزاء في الوجوب الذاتي ويخالفه في التعين فيكون ذلك الشيء مركبا، فلا يكون الواحد واحدا. هذا خلف]
Second: Each of those parts must either be a necessary existent in itself or not. The second division is false, because the composite is dependent on each of its parts. If [any of its parts] is possible in itself, then it is dependent on [that part, and consequently it is in itself dependent on] the possible in itself, and what is dependent on the possible is more worthy of possibility, so it follows that [the necessary in itself would be possible in itself]. That is impossible. Thus it is established that each of those parts is [necessary in itself]. So those parts share in essential necessity and differ by their particularities by virtue of which they differ from each other, and what is shared is other than what is different. Consequently, each of those parts would be composed [of two other parts, and the discussion about those parts is like the first, so it follows that each of them is composed] of infinite parts, and that is impossible. Because every multiplicity, finite or infinite, [the one exists therein, but whatever thing is true, reason judges that it is one, yet it is not one in itself, because that one must share with the other parts in essential necessity and differ from them by individuation, so that thing would be composite, and thus the one would not be one. This is a contradiction.]
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is Razi's second objection against the possibility that a supposed necessary being composed of shared necessity and individuating particularity could exist. The objection proceeds as follows: The parts of such a composite must themselves be either necessary in themselves or not. If any part is possible, then the composite depends on a possible thing and becomes possible, contradicting necessity. Therefore each part must be necessary in itself. But then these parts also share in necessity and differ by particularities, so each part is itself composite in the same way. This reasoning leads to an infinite regress of parts—either finite or infinite—but in any case, the 'one' that is supposed to be a simple necessary being would be composite, and thus not truly one. Hence the original assumption of multiple necessary existents is false.

Key Terms
أجزاء
كثرة
غير متناهية
الواحد
وجوب ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is always a valid argument against any composition.
Correction: Razi's argument targets a specific case: a necessary existent composed of necessity and particularity. Infinite regress is problematic here because it undermines the simplicity required for a necessary being. It does not rule out infinite regress in other contexts like causal chains.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the second objection?
A) The parts of a composite necessary existent must each be possible.
B) The composite would have infinite parts and thus not be truly one.
C) The philosophers' argument is valid for finite composites.
D) The necessary existent cannot have any parts at all.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second refutation shows that assuming multiple necessary existents leads to an infinite regress of parts, contradicting the necessary being's simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's argument shows that anything composite cannot be truly one and cannot be necessary. In your life, are there 'composite' objects of worship or ultimate concerns—things you treat as necessary but which are actually made up of parts and depend on other things? How can you break down these false gods and recenter your heart on the simple, necessary One?
?
The argument reveals that any form of multiplicity in the necessary being is absurd. Reflect on your own intentions: Are your actions for Allah truly single-minded, or are they mixed with multiple motives (rizq, reputation, etc.)? How can this lesson help you purify your intentions so that your worship is directed to the One who is truly one?
?
If the necessary existent must be absolutely simple, then nothing else can share in its necessity. Does your trust (tawakkul) reflect this? Do you rely on created things as if they were necessary? What specific steps can you take today to shift your reliance from created 'parts' to the simple, all-sufficient Creator?

The First Argument Against Two Necessary Existents
Reductio ad absurdum against two necessary existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a reductio ad absurdum argument proves that there cannot be two necessary existents.

Core Idea
Two necessary existents would render each other possible—a contradiction.

The first argument against the possibility of two necessary existents is a concise reductio ad absurdum. It begins by assuming the opposite—two necessary beings—and shows that this assumption leads to a logical contradiction. This argument is foundational in establishing the uniqueness of the Necessary Being (Allah). Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'farḍ' (assumption) and 'bāṭil' (false).
2
Identify the assumption and the contradiction
The passage assumes two necessary existents. It then claims each would also be possible. Ask yourself: why is that a contradiction?
Necessary means cannot not exist; possible means can exist or not. A thing cannot be both.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the assumption leads to a contradiction, it is false and impossible. The existence of two necessary existents is impossible.
This argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum (proof by contradiction).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه ممكن، فثبت أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته، لوجب كون كل واحد منهما ممكنا لذاته، وما أفضى ثبوته إلى عدمه كان باطلا، فيثبت أن فرض موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته: فرض باطل محال، فيثبت أن وجود موجودين واجبي الوجود قول باطل.
For then it is possible; thus it is established that if we assume two existents, each of which is necessary in itself, then each of them would have to be possible in itself. And whatever leads to its own negation is false. Thus it is established that assuming two existents, each of which is necessary in itself, is a false and impossible assumption. Thus it is established that the existence of two necessarily existent beings is a false statement.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by contradiction against the possibility of two necessary existents. It begins by stating the assumption: suppose there are two existents, each necessary in itself. If each is necessary in itself, then each would also be possible in itself—because the existence of one does not necessitate the other. But a thing cannot be both necessary and possible in the same respect. Therefore the assumption leads to a contradiction, so it is impossible. The argument concludes that the existence of two necessary existents is false.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
فرض
باطل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument merely asserts impossibility without proof.
Correction: The argument uses a precise reductio ad absurdum: it assumes the opposite and derives a logical contradiction, thereby proving the impossibility.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude about assuming two necessary existents?
A) It is possible but unlikely.
B) It is a false and impossible assumption.
C) It leads to three necessary existents.
D) It is the basis for polytheism.

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that assuming two necessary existents leads to an inherent contradiction, proving the impossibility of multiple necessary beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been something in your life that you treated as 'necessary'—something you felt you could not live without—that actually competes with the absolute necessity of Allah? What would it mean for you to truly believe that He alone is the Necessary Being and everything else is merely possible?
?
The argument shows that two necessary existents would each be possible because they are independent of each other. Reflect on your own dependencies. Are there people or possessions you rely on as if they were necessary? How can you shift that reliance to Allah alone, recognizing that only He is truly independent?
?
If you truly internalize that the existence of two necessary beings is impossible, how does that affect your confidence in tawheed? Does your heart still waver when faced with difficulties, as if another power could harm or benefit you? What practical step can you take today to strengthen your conviction that Allah is the only Necessary Existence?

Second Argument Against Composition
Second argument refuting composition in the necessary being

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against composition in the necessary being, demonstrating why composition leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
If the necessary being had parts, each part would itself be composite, leading to infinite regress.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument that the necessary being (Allah) cannot be composed of parts. The argument uses a logical dichotomy and a regress to show that composition is incompatible with necessary existence. Follow the steps carefully to see how each part would require its own composition, ultimately resulting in a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation without attempting to analyze it deeply. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
The argument is dense; do not worry if you don't grasp it all at once.
2
Identify the initial dichotomy
The argument starts with a split: each part of the composite necessary being is either necessary in itself or not. Notice how this framing sets up the entire proof.
The second option (not necessary) is immediately shown to be false.
3
Understand why a possible part is impossible
If a part were possible (not necessary), the whole would depend on that part, making the whole possible – contradicting its necessity. This is a key step.
The reasoning relies on dependency: the composite depends on its parts.
4
See the consequence: each part must be necessary
Since possible parts are ruled out, each part must be necessary in itself. But then they share necessity while differing in their specific determinations.
This leads to the next problem: each part becomes composite itself.
5
Recognize the infinite regress and final contradiction
Each part, having a shared element (necessity) and a distinguishing element (determination), is itself composed, leading to an infinite regress. Moreover, the supposed 'one' entity cannot be truly one because it must both share and differ.
The argument concludes that composition is absurd for the necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: إنه إما أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء واجب الوجود لذاته، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك والقسم الثاني باطل، لأن المركب مفتقر إلى كل واحد من أجزائه، فلو كان شيء من أجزائه ممكنا لذاته أنه يكون مفتقرا إلى ذلك الجزء، لزم كونه في ذاته مفتقرا إلى الممكن لذاته، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته ممكنا لذاته. وذلك محال فيثبت: أن كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء يكون واجبا لذاته، فتلك الأجزاء متشاركة في الوجوب الذاتي، ومتباينة بخصوصياتها التي باعتبارها يخالف بعضها بعضا، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة، فيلزم كون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء مركبا من جزئين آخرين، والكلام في تلك الأجزاء كما في الأول، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما مركبا من أجزاء غير متناهية وذلك محال. لأن كل كثرة متناهية أو غير متناهية فإن الواحد فيها موجود إلا أن أي شيء صدق، يحكم العقل بأنه واحد وهذا في نفسه ليس بواحد، لأن ذلك الواحد لابد وأن يشارك سائر الأجزاء في الوجوب الذاتي ويخالفه في التعين فيكون ذلك الشيء مركبا، فلا يكون الواحد واحدا. هذا خلف.
Secondly: Either each one of those parts is necessary in itself, or it is not so; and the second division is false because the composite is dependent upon each one of its parts. If something of its parts were possible in itself, then it would be dependent upon that part, then it would be in itself dependent upon what is possible in itself, and that which is dependent upon the possible is more deserving of possibility, so it would follow that the necessary in itself is possible in itself. That is absurd. Thus it is established that each one of those parts is necessary in itself. So those parts share in self-necessity and differ by their particularities by virtue of which they differ from each other. That by which they share is other than that by which they differ. Thus it follows that each one of those parts is composed of two other parts, and the discussion about those parts is as before. Hence it follows that each one of them is composed of infinite parts, and that is absurd. Because every multiplicity, finite or infinite, has unity in it; but whatever it is, the intellect judges that it is one while it is not one in itself, because that one must share with the rest of the parts in self-necessity and differ in determination, so that thing becomes composite, and the one is not one. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against composition in the necessary being. It begins with a dichotomy: every part of a composite necessary being is either necessary in itself or not. The second alternative is invalid because the composite depends on its parts; if a part were possible, the whole would depend on a possible, making the necessary being itself possible—a contradiction. Therefore each part must be necessary. Then, since these parts share necessity but differ in their particularities, each part is itself composed of a shared element (necessity) and a distinguishing element (determination). This leads to an infinite regress of composition, which is absurd. Moreover, if the composite were a unity, it would have to be both shared and distinct, which is contradictory. Hence the necessary being cannot be composite.

Key Terms
أجزاء
مركب
وجوب ذاتي
مشاركة
مخالفة
تعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that composition is impossible for any being, not just the necessary being.
Correction: The argument specifically targets the necessary being, relying on the concept of being necessary in itself. It does not claim that contingent beings cannot be composite.

Checkpoint
What follows if a part of the necessary being were possible in itself?
A) The part would be independent.
B) The whole would be possible in itself.
C) The whole would become necessary.
D) The part would become necessary.

Key Takeaway
The necessary being cannot have parts because composition leads to an infinite regress or a contradiction about unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the God you worship is absolutely simple and indivisible, with no 'parts' of self-necessity and determination? How does that affect the way you direct your devotion? If you find yourself splitting your attention between Allah and other things, what would it take to unify your worship entirely toward Him?
?
The argument shows that any composition leads to infinite regress. In your spiritual life, are there beliefs or practices that you have built on a foundation of many 'parts' that are not rooted in the pure, simple truth of tawheed? What would you need to strip away to make your faith indivisible?
?
If you were asked to defend the oneness of Allah using the reasoning in this passage, which part of the argument would challenge your own understanding most? How does the idea that a necessary being cannot depend on any part confront your assumptions about God?

Third Argument Against Composition from Necessity and Determination
Third argument refuting composition via three hypothetical relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument that refutes the idea that the necessary being is composed of necessity and determination by examining three possible relationships between them.

Core Idea
Composition of necessity and determination leads to contradictions in all three possible scenarios.

Having established that the necessary being cannot be composite, Muslim theologians offered several arguments. This passage presents the third argument, focusing on two aspects: necessity (shared) and determination (distinctive). It examines three possible relationships between these parts and shows each leads to impossibility, thereby reinforcing the simplicity of the necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the structure of the argument.
Notice the three divisions marked by 'فإما أن يكون' and 'وأما...فنقول'.
2
Identify the three possible relationships
Find the three cases: (1) necessity entails determination, (2) determination entails necessity, (3) neither entails the other. Note how each is introduced.
These correspond to the first, second, and third divisions.
3
Analyze why each case is impossible
For each case, follow the reasoning that leads to a contradiction: case1 leads to only one necessary being; case2 makes necessity dependent; case3 requires an external cause.
The conclusion for each case is explicitly called 'باطل' (false) or 'محال' (absurd).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
After all three are refuted, the only possibility is that the necessary being is not composed of necessity and determination. This supports the doctrine of tawhid.
The argument does not assume the existence of multiple necessary beings; it refutes composition in any necessary being.

والوجه الثالث في إبطال كون كل واحد منها مركبا من الوجوب الذي به تحصل المشاركة ومن التعين الذي به تحصل المخالفة: هو أنه لما كان كل واحد منها مركبا من هذين الجزئين، فإما أن يكون الوجوب الذي به المشاركة يستلزم ذلك التعين لعينه، وإما أن يكون ذلك التعين الذي به المخالفة يستلزم ذلك الوجوب الذي به المشاركة أو أن يكون كل واحد منهما غير مستلزم للثاني، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة. أما الأول وهو أن الوجوب الذي به المشاركة يستلزم ذلك التعين الذي به المخالفة. فنقول: إن كان الأمر كذلك، فإينا حصل الوجوب بالذات، وجب أن يحصل ذلك التعين وكل ما كان واجب الوجود لذاته، وجب أن يكون هو ذلك المعين، فحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا ذلك الواحد، وأما الثاني وهو أن يقال: إن ذلك التعين يوجب الوجوب بالذات، فهذا أيضاً باطل، لأن الوجوب بالذات إذا كان معللا بذلك التعين، كان الوجوب بالذات واجبا بغيره، وكل ما كان واجبا بغيره كان ممكنا لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات ممكنا لذاته. وذلك متناقض. وأما الثالث وهو أن يقال: الوجوب بالذات لا يستلزم ذلك الجزء. وذلك التعين أيضا لا يستلزم الوجوب بالذات، فحينئذ يكون اجتماعها لا بد وأن يكون بسبب منفصل، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته واجبا بغيره. وذلك محال.
The third aspect in refuting that each one of them is composed of the necessity by which participation occurs and the determination by which difference occurs: is that since each one of them is composed of these two parts, either the necessity by which participation occurs entails that determination itself, or that determination by which difference occurs entails that necessity by which participation occurs, or each one of them does not entail the other. All three divisions are false. As for the first, that the necessity by which participation occurs entails that determination by which difference occurs, we say: if it is so, then wherever self-necessity obtains, that determination must obtain, and whatever is necessary in itself must be that determined thing, and then the necessary in itself is only that one. As for the second, that it is said that that determination necessitates self-necessity, this too is false, because if self-necessity is caused by that determination, then self-necessity would be necessary through another, and whatever is necessary through another is possible in itself, so it would follow that self-necessity is possible in itself. That is contradictory. As for the third, that it is said that self-necessity does not entail that part, and that determination also does not entail self-necessity, then their coming together must be due to a separate cause, so it would follow that the necessary in itself is necessary through another. That is absurd.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against composition in the necessary being. It assumes that if the necessary being had parts, those parts would be necessity (shared) and determination (distinguishing). It then examines three possible relationships between them: (1) necessity entails determination, (2) determination entails necessity, (3) neither entails the other. All three lead to contradiction. If necessity entails determination, then wherever there is necessity there is that specific determination, so only one determined being is necessary, eliminating multiplicity. If determination entails necessity, then necessity would be caused by determination, making necessity dependent and thus possible—contradiction to self-necessity. If neither entails, then their combination requires an external cause, making the necessary being dependent on another—again impossible. Hence composition of necessity and determination is impossible.

تعين
مشاركة
مخالفة
استلزام
واجب بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies if the necessary being is composite; it is a general refutation of composition.
Correction: The argument assumes a particular composite structure (necessity + determination) and shows it leads to contradictions, thus refuting that specific type of composition in the necessary being.

Checkpoint
What follows if necessity entails determination in the composite necessary being?
A) Necessity becomes possible.
B) There would be only one necessary being.
C) An infinite regress occurs.
D) The parts are independent.

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that any composition of necessity and determination leads to logical impossibilities, reinforcing the simplicity of the necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Necessary Being cannot be composed of parts, what does that imply about your mental image of God? Are you willing to let go of any anthropomorphic or composite picture you have held?
?
The argument shows that any form of dependence (being necessary through another) is impossible for the truly necessary. In your daily life, what created things do you rely on that you should not? How can you shift your reliance to the simple Necessary alone?
?
Consider case 2: if determination entailed necessity, then self-necessity would be caused by determination. Do you ever treat God's attributes as if they were separate causes of His essence? How does the argument correct that assumption in your heart?

Fourth Argument: The Determined Essence Is Not Composite
Fourth argument proving the determined essence of the necessary being is not composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the trilemma argument proving that the necessary being cannot be composite and that its determination must be identical to or caused by its essence.

Core Idea
The necessary being must be one with its determination or depend on something else.

In this fourth argument, Imam Razi presents a powerful trilemma that forces us to consider the relationship between the necessary being and its determination. By eliminating all but one option, he demonstrates that the necessary being must be simple and non-composite, upholding its absolute necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice the trilemma is presented as three possibilities.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The argument offers three options: the necessary being is (1) identical to its determination, (2) the cause of its determination, or (3) neither. Locate each in the text.
3
Analyze the implications of each possibility
For options (1) and (2), note that they lead to oneness. For option (3), work through the reasoning: if determination comes from an external cause, the necessary being becomes possible in itself.
This is the key logical move.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since option (3) leads to a contradiction (necessary being being possible), it is invalid. Therefore only (1) or (2) are valid, both implying the necessary being is one and not composite.
The argument refutes composition in essence, not attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع في إبطال أن يقال أن الذات المتعينة مركبة: هو أن نقول: واجب الوجود إما أن يكون هو نفس ذلك التعين، أو يكون موجبا لذلك التعين، أولا يكون نفس ذلك التعين، ولا موجبا له والقسمان الأولان يوجبان أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته واحدا، والقسم الثالث يقتضي أن لا يصير واجب الوجود لذاته متعينا إلا بسبب منفصل، وما لا تثبت هويته إلا بسبب منفصل، كان ممكنا لذاته فيلزم أن يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، ممكن الوجود لذاته، وذلك محال.
The fourth aspect in refuting that it is said that the determined essence is composite: is that we say: the necessary existent is either the very determination itself, or it is a cause of that determination, or it is neither the determination itself nor a cause of it. The first two divisions require that the necessary in itself be one, and the third division necessitates that the necessary in itself does not become determined except by a separate cause, and whatever's identity is not established except by a separate cause is possible in itself, so it would follow that the necessary in itself is possible in itself, and that is absurd.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against composition in the necessary being, focusing on the relationship between the necessary existent and its determination. It offers a trilemma: the necessary existent is either (1) identical to its determination, (2) the cause of its determination, or (3) neither. The first two options imply that the necessary existent is one (since determination is unique), upholding unity. The third option leads to a problem: if the necessary existent becomes determined only by an external cause, then its identity depends on something else, making it possible in itself—contradicting its necessity. Therefore, the only valid conclusion is that the necessary existent is one and not composite.

Key Terms
ذات متعينة
تعين
موجب
هوية
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies the reality of divine attributes or distinctions within God.
Correction: The argument only addresses composition in the essence (that the necessary being is made of parts). It does not negate attributes that are identical to the essence.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude if the necessary existent is neither identical to its determination nor its cause?
A) It is independent.
B) It would be possible in itself.
C) It becomes composite.
D) It is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The necessary being must be one with its determination; otherwise, its necessity would be compromised.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's very identity is inseparable from His determination, what does that say about your own identity? Are you defined by something external and changeable, or by your relationship with the Necessary Being?
?
The argument shows that if God's determination came from an outside cause, He would be contingent. Do you treat God as if He is dependent on your worship or actions? How can you realign your heart to see Him as utterly self-sufficient?
?
The necessary being is non-composite: there is no division in His essence. Is your own devotion to Him divided? Where do you feel a split between what you profess and how you live, and what step can you take today to unify your intention for His sake alone?

First Refutation: Necessity Entails Determination
First refutation: necessity entails determination leads to unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that necessity entails determination leads to the conclusion that all necessary existents are identical, thereby refuting plurality.

Core Idea
If necessity forces determination, then all necessary existents collapse into one.

This lesson tackles the first of three possible relationships between necessity and determination. The opponent may argue that the necessity shared by hypothetical multiple necessary existents entails the specific determination that makes them distinct. We will follow the logical consequences of this claim and see how it self-destructs.

Guided Path
1
Read the first refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the conditional reasoning: if necessity entails determination, then wherever necessity exists, that determination must also exist.
Notice the author uses a direct conditional: 'فإينا حصل الوجوب بالذات وجب أن يحصل ذلك التعين'.
2
Identify the logical outcome
Trace the conclusion: all things that are necessary in themselves would be that same determined entity, so there can be only one.
3
Reflect on the contradiction with plurality
Consider that this argument shows the impossibility of multiple necessary existents if they share necessity and differ by determination.
This is a crucial step in proving the oneness of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك الوجوب الذي به المشاركة أو أن يكون كل واحد منهما غير مستلزم للثاني، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة. أما الأول وهو أن الوجوب الذي به المشاركة يستلزم ذلك التعين الذي به المخالفة. فنقول: إن كان الأمر كذلك، فإينا حصل الوجوب بالذات وجب أن يحصل ذلك التعين، وكل ما كان واجب الوجود لذاته وجب أن يكون هو ذلك المعين، فحينئذ يكون واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا ذلك الواحد. وأما الثاني وهو أن يقال: إن ذلك التعين يوجب الوجوب بالذات، فهذا أيضاً باطل، لأن الوجوب بالذات إذا كان معللا بذلك التعين، كان الوجوب بالذات واجبا بغيره، وكل ما كان واجبا بغيره كان ممكنا لذاته، فيلزم أن يكون الوجوب بالذات ممكنا لذاته. وذلك متناقض. وأما الثالث وهو أن يقال: الوجوب بالذات لا يستلزم ذلك الجزء، وذلك التعين أيضا لا يستلزم الوجوب بالذات، فحينئذ يكون اجتماعها لا بد وأن يكون بسبب منفصل، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته واجبا بغيره. وذلك محال.
The necessity by which there is participation, or that each of the two does not entail the other, and the three divisions are invalid. As for the first: that the necessity by which there is participation entails that determination by which there is difference. We say: if it is so, then whenever necessity in itself occurs, that determination must occur, and everything that is necessary of existence in itself must be that determined [entity]; then the necessary of existence in itself is nothing but that one. As for the second: that it is said that that determination necessitates necessity in itself; this is also invalid, because if necessity in itself is caused by that determination, then necessity in itself would be necessary by another, and everything that is necessary by another is possible in itself, so it would follow that necessity in itself is possible in itself, and that is contradictory. As for the third: that it is said that necessity in itself does not entail that part, and that determination does not entail necessity in itself; then their conjunction must be due to a separate cause, so it would follow that the necessary in itself is necessary by another, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three possible relationships between the necessity of existence (wujub al-dhat) and the specific determination (ta'ayyun) that distinguishes one necessary existent from another. The context assumes an opponent who posits a plurality of necessary existents sharing a common necessity but differing by determinations. The author examines three logical possibilities: (1) necessity entails determination, (2) determination entails necessity, (3) neither entails the other. All three are shown to be invalid. In the first case, if necessity entails determination, then wherever necessity exists, that particular determination must exist, forcing all necessary existents to be identical – which contradicts plurality. In the second, if determination causes necessity, then necessity would be caused, making it necessary through another, which implies it is possible in itself – a contradiction because necessity in itself cannot be contingent. In the third, if neither entails the other, then their conjunction requires an external cause, again making the necessary in itself dependent on another and thus contingent. By eliminating all three, the author indirectly supports the oneness of the Necessary Existent.

Key Terms
الوجوب بالذات (wujub bi-dhat)
التعين (ta'ayyun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that multiple necessary existents can share the same necessity but differ by their intrinsic determinations without logical conflict.
Correction: This passage shows that if necessity entails determination, then all such existents would be identical, disproving the possibility of plurality.

Checkpoint
What conclusion follows if the necessity shared by necessary existents entails their specific determination?
A) There would be many necessary existents.
B) All necessary existents would be identical.
C) Necessity would be accidental.
D) Determination would be independent.

Key Takeaway
The claim that necessity entails determination forces all necessary existents to be identical, refuting the possibility of plurality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the only necessary existent, how does that demand your entire life be oriented towards Him alone? In what areas do you still act as if there are other 'necessary' sources of security or meaning? Commit today to one area you will surrender fully.
?
The argument shows that any necessary existent must be uniquely determined. How does this confront your tendency to compartmentalize your faith, treating the Oneness of Allah as a theoretical belief rather than the defining reality of every moment? What specific change will you make today to live this unity?
?
Consider that if necessity entails determination, then nothing else can share in that necessity. Does your heart truly recognize that only Allah is self-sufficient? What created thing do you still rely on as if it were necessary? Make a concrete plan to wean yourself from that reliance.
